        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer971536214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer678508419599235796">070819: i just read of the Boy Scouts of America being sued for sexual abuse history, this made me remember durant’s enthusiasm for the catholic church as plato’s republic... and we have much proof of how it has been home to years (decades? centuries?) of same sort of abuse. this is hard to read, hard to agree, that there is ever such incorruptible pedagogical model... not now, not ever... <br/><br/>140914 first review: according to GR this is the 300th philosophy book I have read (read 593 according to t</span>
  <span id="freeText678508419599235796" style="display:none">070819: i just read of the Boy Scouts of America being sued for sexual abuse history, this made me remember durant’s enthusiasm for the catholic church as plato’s republic... and we have much proof of how it has been home to years (decades? centuries?) of same sort of abuse. this is hard to read, hard to agree, that there is ever such incorruptible pedagogical model... not now, not ever... <br /><br />140914 first review: according to GR this is the 300th philosophy book I have read (read 593 according to this, 634 according to home list 070819 i have no idea why big diff). this is a book that my father read when he was about 14 or 15, in high school, though it was first published in 1926, was already known, approved, recommended, and this was probably enough philosophy for him. he thought to become an engineer (philosophy was too ‘abstract’) he became a ‘theoretical chemist’ (or ‘chemical physicist9:’ or ‘physical chemist’... a professor of abstract physics/chemistry anyway), instead. so in reading this book I am fortunate to have conversations with the absent- the author long dead, my father far away- and there are at least three books I read in one... this is also an example of a certain intellectual loyalty to my father, in that i only read this by now, though this was significant to him- I do not have to argue with him about it...<br /><br />we ran out of books I had not read, while in Waimea, so I took this copy a friend had given my father some years back. this is also the main philosophy my father has read, except for some Spinoza and William James. read 164 pages on the flight, though I might have nodded off because the trip seemed shorter. this covered Plato, Aristotle, Bacon, Spinoza, some Voltaire. of whom I am somewhat familiar, but not enough to critique, except perhaps disagreeing with the author's great enthusiasm for the Plato's republic manifest by the medieval Catholic Church... generally, I was pleased, I was reading this as written- an ambitious history of western philosophy, with caveats, though perhaps tending to concepts of the author's time, eg. tendencies of thought peculiar to this or that nationality, a general tendency to link not quite deterministically the society, times, psychology, of the chosen philosopher, with the philosophy summarized. easy to read though, even if I was more interested in the thoughts than details of lives and times. had to think of what this would have been for a young, smart, poor, farm boy growing up on the wrong side of the tracks (my father)... the general idea that the world was open to intellectual analysis, that ideas could create that world, that some concepts that led to science and intelligence, rather than that heaving sea of intuition that seemed to drown as much as carry humans (his mother dying of Parkinson's, his father working and working, his sisters being emotional artist types), these ideas must have been heartening for him. rather less so for me, but more in the brilliance and horror in the years since written, it remains a pleasant hope...<br /><br />yesterday, in meeting friends not seen for three weeks, there was only 100 pages read, but here and then more so today, I started to have problems with this book. read all today, so this is first impression. this was yesterday: Kant, Hegel, Schopenhauer: today Spencer, Nietzsche, then European, then American philosophers contemporaneous to the author. well here is when i began to drift away from the line of his history, such as the rather cursory summation, psychological denigration, and dismissal of Kant and those German idealists, such as the impenetrable (to Durant...) therefore meaningless guy Hegel, well he is given a few pages... felt he was beginning to weigh heavily on psychology, particularly on the pessimism of Schopenhauer being a reaction to failures and horrors of the century- French Revolution, Napoleon, Seven Years War- but he was still moving quickly, perhaps I am judging too harshly... Spencer, though? basically, Durant has nothing good to say about Continental thinkers, who happen to greatly interest me, but he is getting closer to his time, he is talking about (then) living philosophers, but the conception of philosophy he worked with is exactly the kind of thinking i believe rather opposite the ideas of Husserl and his phenomenology followers... Durant's conception of philosophy seem to be that the person is only ‘subjective’ centre of life, therefore not important, that it is the ‘objective’ that is true and that natural science is the way to conceive everything from a very high altitude.. the idea all metaphysics are resolved, that all political thought should just give up and look for Philosopher Kings and set up the Republic, again, again, again...<br /><br />so I liked it, less at the end than beginning, but I can see why it was written this way- popular psychology rather than philosophy- can see how it influenced my father, can see how it is for me. not bad, but better than just ok...</span>
  <a data-text-id="678508419599235796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2485636313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14965858407817549988"><i>Philosophy is to be understood as experience and thought, not as mere reading or passive study</i><br/>I was embarked on an incredible journey by Will Durant. A reflection on philosopher's life made it a very interesting read. Strongly recommended particularly to those who are disinclined to philosophy because of the misconception that it is boring<br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_14946262" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14946262" />
    <a title="Paulius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/847862-paulius"><img alt="Paulius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201588122p2/847862.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14946262?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paulius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paulius" href="/user/show/847862-paulius">Paulius</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer14946262&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14946262" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7021487008218583435">The Story of Philosophy (1926)<br/><br/>Will Durant’s Story of Philosophy is a must-read volume. It is not a history of philosophy in the world, but stories of dominant personalities in western philosophy through their lives and work. He includes sections on Plato, Aristotle, Francis Bacon, Spinoza, Voltaire, Kant, Schopenhauer, Spencer, Nietzsche, Bergson, Croce, Bertrand Russell, Santayana, William James, and his own teacher at Columbia University, John Dewey.<br/><br/>His joy of Plato’s style is expressed when h</span>
  <span id="freeText7021487008218583435" style="display:none">The Story of Philosophy (1926)<br /><br />Will Durant’s Story of Philosophy is a must-read volume. It is not a history of philosophy in the world, but stories of dominant personalities in western philosophy through their lives and work. He includes sections on Plato, Aristotle, Francis Bacon, Spinoza, Voltaire, Kant, Schopenhauer, Spencer, Nietzsche, Bergson, Croce, Bertrand Russell, Santayana, William James, and his own teacher at Columbia University, John Dewey.<br /><br />His joy of Plato’s style is expressed when he says, “the difficulty in understanding Plato lies precisely in this intoxicating mixture of philosophy and poetry, of science and art.” {p. 14} Namely, Plato as poet can be fun to read. Durant is amazed by the prodigious accomplishments of Aristotle but admits, “We must not expect of Aristotle such literary brilliance as floods the pages of the dramatist-philosopher Plato. Instead of giving us great literature, in which philosophy is embodied (and obscured) in myth and imagery, Aristotle gives us science, technical, abstract, concentrated: if we go to him for entertainment we shall sue for the return of our money.” {p. 46} With Francis Bacon, we have the ultimate blend of theory and practice that I begin to accept as an ideal for myself, for Bacon “felt that studies could not be either end or wisdom in themselves, and that knowledge unapplied in action was a pale academic vanity.” {p. 86} Spinoza was Durant’s first love in the literature of philosophy. He was so gentle and wise that if only the public could read and understand his message “perhaps in his name a world torn with suicidal wars of tongue and sword might find a unity of faith and a possibility of brotherhood at last.” {p. 127} Durant made me a believer in the incredible gifts of Voltaire and part of my appreciation of this Enlightenment genius is his staunch defense of tolerance where “the very first step towards social health was the destruction of the ecclesiastical power in which intolerance had its root.” {p. 180} And then there’s the incomparable wit of Voltaire. When Rousseau sent him a copy of a “back to nature” type of book he had written, with which Voltaire heartily disagreed, the master wit wrote back to him, “I have received, sir, your new book against the human species, and I thank you for it….No one has ever been so witty as you are in trying to turn us into brutes; to read your book makes one long to go on all fours. As, however, it is now some sixty years since I gave up the practice, I feel that it is unfortunately impossible for me to resume it.” {p. 188} Durant displays a talent for telling stories with many remarkable phrases we can treasure. In one of his essays, Francis Bacon wrote, “Some books are to be tasted, others to be swallowed, and some few to be chewed and digested.” {p. 87} This book of Durant’s deserves to be chewed and digested.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7021487008218583435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1179500448" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1179500448" />
    <a title="Alex Obrigewitsch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32693205-alex-obrigewitsch"><img alt="Alex Obrigewitsch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404171519p2/32693205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1179500448?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Obrigewitsch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Obrigewitsch" href="/user/show/32693205-alex-obrigewitsch">Alex Obrigewitsch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1179500448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13806621828464664134">I had a difficult time finishing this book. It suffers from the same problems that every history of philosophy that I have ever read suffers from, that being that it is tainted by the agenda and the opinion of its writer.<br/>This is clearly manifest in the author's choices (no Descartes?), as well as his at times sketchy or selectively purposive interpretations (I'm thinking specifically of Nietzsche, as well as the offhand dismissal of Hegel).<br/>The problem with these histories is that they very often</span>
  <span id="freeText13806621828464664134" style="display:none">I had a difficult time finishing this book. It suffers from the same problems that every history of philosophy that I have ever read suffers from, that being that it is tainted by the agenda and the opinion of its writer.<br />This is clearly manifest in the author's choices (no Descartes?), as well as his at times sketchy or selectively purposive interpretations (I'm thinking specifically of Nietzsche, as well as the offhand dismissal of Hegel).<br />The problem with these histories is that they very often taint the thinking of those fresh to philosophy, by telling them what a philosopher is about, rather than having them struggle through the philosopher's work to find their own understanding, and subsequently to form their own thinking. This is what philosophy is for, in some ways, and so histories such as this affect thinking negatively overall.<br />Durant attempts to popularize philosophy, but if it is not popular because it is difficult, then the answer is not to simplify it, but to disregard those who are unwilling to rise to the challenge and grow through the attempt.<br />Don't read this book. Instead, pick up Plato, or Nietzsche, or any other philosopher you are interested in. Turn to the writer and thinker themselves, and not to someone else's interpretation. Second-hand thought is a step on the road to no thought at all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13806621828464664134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10375943830107463903">A few quick comments about this amazing book:<br/><br/>* It is a shame it was written in the 20's and misses many great philosophical movements since that time, as I would love to know Durant's take on trends such as existentialism and feminism.<br/><br/>* Durant seems to have a much greater interest in the early philosophers than in more recent ones.   <span id="freeText10375943830107463903" style="display:none">A few quick comments about this amazing book:<br /><br />* It is a shame it was written in the 20's and misses many great philosophical movements since that time, as I would love to know Durant's take on trends such as existentialism and feminism.<br /><br />* Durant seems to have a much greater interest in the early philosophers than in more recent ones. The book became somewhat less engrossing after the French Enlightenment chapter. I don't know if that was my fault or the author's.<br /><br />* I would recommend this more as a biographical look at the philosophers than a chronology of philosophical thought from Plato to Santayana. That being said, Durant makes this a fun and enlightening read. Often times his editorials, though subtle, add valuable insight into the ideas being discussed.<br /><br />* After reading this I am tempted to embark upon Bertrand Russell's History of Western Philosophy to compare the two--but my ambition may be lacking...<br /> </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3308459837" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3308459837" />
    <a title="Sajith Kumar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30281185-sajith-kumar"><img alt="Sajith Kumar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452522694p2/30281185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3308459837?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sajith Kumar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sajith Kumar" href="/user/show/30281185-sajith-kumar">Sajith Kumar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30281185-sajith-kumar?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3308459837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9607032301495161989">The term ‘philosophy’ has something repulsive in the nature it sounds for most of us mortals. That is because it is most of the time mixed with obscure metaphysics that lives only in the abstract domain. This book was with me unopened for about 25 years. But when the unexpected Covid lockdown dried up my reservoir of books, there was no other option than seeking refuge in the lap of the good old dusty volume sitting in the corner of my bookshelf. It took much time to complete, but now I am not s</span>
  <span id="freeText9607032301495161989" style="display:none">The term ‘philosophy’ has something repulsive in the nature it sounds for most of us mortals. That is because it is most of the time mixed with obscure metaphysics that lives only in the abstract domain. This book was with me unopened for about 25 years. But when the unexpected Covid lockdown dried up my reservoir of books, there was no other option than seeking refuge in the lap of the good old dusty volume sitting in the corner of my bookshelf. It took much time to complete, but now I am not so averse to philosophy. I don’t pretend that I have understood it all, but what I had assimilated after reading the book has spawned a new respect for philosophy. Philosophy is in fact organized thinking about the fundamental nature of the world, society and man. Queries on god and soul originate as a corollary to the serious researcher, but there is enough to satisfy an inquisitive lay reader in this book which presents the great philosophers from Plato to John Dewey and the system of knowledge they promulgated. Philosophy attempts to coordinate the real in the light of the ideal. It begins when one learns to doubt, particularly to doubt one’s cherished beliefs, dogmas and axioms. Will Durant was an American writer, historian and philosopher who was best known for his work ‘The Story of Civilization’ in eleven volumes. ‘The Story of Philosophy’ is widely described as a groundbreaking work that helped to popularize philosophy.<br /><br />Durant chalks out his role as a ground-breaking exercise. The difference between philosophy and science is first of all clearly established. Philosophy deals with problems that are not yet open to the methods of science like good and evil, order and freedom. As soon as a field of enquiry yields knowledge susceptible to exact formulation, it is called science. The role of an interpreter to smoothen the ride of the novices is also spelt out. If knowledge becomes too great for communication, it would degenerate into scholasticism and mankind would slip into a new age of faith, worshipping its new priests at a respectful distance. A teacher mediates between the specialist and the nation, learning the specialist’s technical jargon and breaks down the barriers between knowledge and need. The author dons that mantle for us.<br /><br />Of the varied characteristics of the philosophers mentioned in the book, the readers can quickly follow the arguments of the ancient masters such as Plato and Aristotle than more recent ones. Plato had such an avid concern for philosophy that he wanted it to be taught first only after the student has reached 30 years of age; that too, after eliminating a great many through a selection process. The philosophers were never fond of socialism and would have recoiled in horror at the thought of ‘simplifying’ their work for the consumption of the masses like what this book does. Aristotle had a far-reaching writ in philosophy through his thoughts. Many of the indispensable terms we now use for philosophical thought such as faculty, maxim, mean, category and energy etc. were minted in Aristotle’s mind. With his death, the world awaited the resurrection of philosophy for a thousand years. His works were translated by Nestorians into Syriac in fifth century CE and thence into Arabic and Hebrew in the tenth century. By the year 1225, its Latin translations were ready, thereby making them accessible to Europe. Crusaders brought back more accurate copies of works. With the fall of Constantinople in 1453, Greek scholars of the city brought further Athenian treasures with them, when they fled from the besieging Turks.<br /><br />The legacy of Aristotle reigned supreme through the Middle Ages till it was challenged by Francis Bacon. He resolved to set philosophy into a more fertile path, to turn it from scholastic disputation to the illumination and increase of human good. As with other philosophers, the democratic spirit did not appeal to Renaissance thinkers as well. Bacon distrusts people, who were in his day without access to education. Philosophers pined for an aristocracy which was entitled to rule. Plato had even called for a philosopher king. <br /><br />The turf of philosophy too witnessed the transition of economic and political processes from feudal aristocracy to the rule of the middle class. Voltaire and Rousseau were the two voices that signaled this process. The first stirrings of dethronement of kings from history were seen in Voltaire’s philosophy of history in its attempt to trace the streams of natural causation in the development of European mind. The nineteenth century saw monarchy give way to universities as the asylum of philosophers.<br /><br />Nietzsche’s philosophy was said to be the theoretical bedrock of autocratic polities of the Nazis and Fascists. Durant gives a faithful narrative of the German scholar’s thought that is sure to upset a modern mind steeped in liberal ideology. Nietzsche argued unblinkingly for the elite among the elite. Because of democracy and Judeo-Christian morals, the strong are now ashamed of their strength and begin to seek reasons for their prominence rather than asserting it unhesitatingly. The whole morality of Europe is based upon the values which are useful only to the herd. The formula for societal decay is that the virtues proper to the herd infecting the leaders and breaking them into common clay. Nietzsche also advocates the rearing of a class of supermen to rule the society. The superman is a superior individual rising out of mass mediocrity by deliberate breeding and careful nurture. They must have good birth in a noble family. Intellect alone does not enable and an element of heredity is also accounted for. They are to be subjected to severe schooling where perfection will be extracted as a matter of course. A man so born and bred would be beyond good and evil. Other people should serve the superman. For Nietzsche, war is an admirable remedy for peoples that are growing weak. War and universal military service are the antidotes to democratic effeminacy. No wonder he was anathema to the progressive mind of the twentieth century.<br /><br />A great drawback of the book is that it is confined to Western philosophy with not even an aerial survey of Indian and Chinese philosophical systems. This is especially jarring as the author finds Indian influence in Spinoza’s remarks such as ‘the greatest good is the knowledge of the union which the mind has with the whole nature’. The misogyny of the reputed philosophers will surprise modern readers. Schopenhauer’s conviction that the most distinguished intellects among the whole [feminine] sex have never managed to produce a single achievement in the fine arts that is really genuine and original; or given to the world any work of permanent value in any sphere (p.343) is a representative sample written in the latter half of nineteenth century. The author also tries to train the readers in properly appreciating philosophy. You should not read a book of philosophy all at once, but in small portions at many sittings. And having finished it, consider that you have begun to understand it. Read then some commentary. Finally, read the book again which will feel like a new one. When you’ve finished it, you will remain forever a lover of philosophy (p.170).<br /><br />Durant has followed as witty a narrative as a book of philosophy can afford. However, I don’t claim that I enjoyed it, though it is equally true that I don’t repent it either. Such books mark a milestone in your reading life rather than providing a quick dose of fleeting pleasure. I regard completing this great work the same as my ‘conquest’ of Arnold Toynbee’s ‘A Study of History’ in eleven volumes (reviewed earlier). This particular volume was especially difficult with its very small typeface for the main text and the still smaller quotes and foot notes.<br /><br />The book is highly recommended for serious readers.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9607032301495161989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3308459837">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2458282753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2458282753" />
    <a title="Phrodrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67951314-phrodrick"><img alt="Phrodrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545868348p2/67951314.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2458282753?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phrodrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phrodrick" href="/user/show/67951314-phrodrick">Phrodrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="trade format paperback 9780786102877" href="/book/show/3815868-the-story-of-philosophy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2458282753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6568973311999677849">Of the several efforts I have made to acquaint myself with the leading western philosophers, Will Durant’s The Story of Philosophy has left me with the least respect for most of these men. The 4 stars are because I think Durant tells his version well enough and I was certainly engaged. It is not his fault if so many of the great minds were disappointing. My recommendation is that you skip this book and select instead: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/931398.The_Western_Intellectual_Tradition_From_Leonardo_to_Hegel' title='The Western Intellectual Tradition From Leonardo to Hegel by Jacob Bronowski' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Western Intellectual Tradition: From Leonardo to Hegel</a>.<br/><br/>When I told one Goo</span>
  <span id="freeText6568973311999677849" style="display:none">Of the several efforts I have made to acquaint myself with the leading western philosophers, Will Durant’s The Story of Philosophy has left me with the least respect for most of these men. The 4 stars are because I think Durant tells his version well enough and I was certainly engaged. It is not his fault if so many of the great minds were disappointing. My recommendation is that you skip this book and select instead: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/931398.The_Western_Intellectual_Tradition_From_Leonardo_to_Hegel" title="The Western Intellectual Tradition From Leonardo to Hegel by Jacob Bronowski" rel="nofollow noopener">The Western Intellectual Tradition: From Leonardo to Hegel</a>.<br /><br />When I told one Good Reads friend I was going to read this book, I was cautioned that Durant can be “middle brow”. I hereby admit that on the subject of philosophy, middle brow may be as much brow as I have to offer. I quoted a selection to another reviewer. One I know to be well educated in philosophy and several steps deeper in his thinking. His reply was that Durant can be full of it. <br /><br />Durant has his enthusiasms and he may have allowed his POV to color what he selects to emphasize. In his introduction (to the second edition) he admits to failing to include any non-Western thinkers and that what is call “The” Story is at most “A” Story. I had not realized that my copy was one previously owned by my mother, but from the looks of it, she only used it to check on some material related to Spinoza.<br /><br />Among the things that most shocked me about so many of the great thinkers herein represented is their determination to over generalize. Almost all reach wild conclusions not just beyond the data, but based on no data. A random insertion of “often”, “occasionally”, “possibly” and similar words would help many of these men sound reasonable. Too often they sound like the men over 30 still living at home; initiating and spreading rumors and conspiracy theory having the pasty skin from avoiding sunlight and having little or no firsthand experience of life beyond the nearest fast food outlet. <br /><br />For a paragraph, let us summarize the men of this book; using their rules for argument. These men all hate women, have collectively no experience of life outside the garret, or classroom. They pontificate on the degraded, feminizing effect of an Eastern World of which their store of real knowledge is nil. Having no first hand notion of living or the complexities of life they have decided that life is driven by conflict, is limited to what we can perceive, and we cannot perceive much; that the urge to procreate keeps us from being much of anything and that death, being as much as anyone can achieve, it is preferable to life. And that is all before we get to the vomitus sloganeering drivel that is Nietzche.<br /><br />I feel better. There is more to it, but these guys need lessons in logical argument. <br />A problem many of these men address is the problem of the “thing in itself” Humans experience life via the senses. Via the senses we can make certain judgements about what we see, hear, touch and so forth. We cannot state with any certainty that what we sense and the words we use to describe what we sense are in absolute fact everything that anything might be. Therefore our senses cannot tell us about the thing in itself and our language makes it certain that what we can communicate about what we have sensed is even less of this greater reality. <br /><br />A scientist would say that: No one know what red looks like. <br /><br />I recently read of a game a person played on his son. Using a piece of string and 3 or 4 measuring devices he ‘proved’ it was not possible to get any 2 of these devices to produce the same answer and therefore, ??? the sting has no length? The demonstration itself is the failure. The margin of error on a run of 4 is higher than the degree of precision he demanded. <br />Had he run the experiment legitimately he would have found one of two results:<br />1 After a much larger number of measurements; the measurements would have centered around one value. This is called Regression to the Mean.<br />2 It is possible that no one value would ever be repeated due to the inaccuracy of the devices. While this is effectively a restatement of the Thing in Itself problem, it is also a matter of rounding error and in science this is called ‘tolerance’. A word I wish the abstract philosophers would ponder. <br /><br />Going back to the problem of red. We cannot know exactly what a person sees when they see red, but we can ask what they think they see. Over time the answers are likely to repeat certain concepts. Artists use red, because they trust that red will evoke a particular set of responses in those who view the art. Market people have learned to bet very large money on this response and mostly the bet is a winner.<br /><br />It takes the Philosophers to Santayana to suggest that whatever the limitation of our senses living is a lot more successful if we trust them. <br /><br />There remains a problem. If we accept our senses there is no reason to keep looking. If we do not keep looking we will not learn more. Failing to learn more means that we may not yet know that thing. But having learned more we are still tied to our senses and our language and must assume that we have failed to understand the thing itself. And so another relatively modern philosopher, Croce admits <br />“I believe, he writes, “That we shall never know exactly why a thing is beautiful" <br /><br />This brings me to a point of mine:<br />Too often the argument is made that the scientist only sees the structure and misses the beauty. As if the scientist would look at Michelangelo ‘s La Pietà and only see the crystalline structure and the kinds of polish and miss its beauty. There may be such a person and perhaps we are right to ignore that person’s esthetic pleasure in the science of things. Can we admit to the possibility that the same person can react to both the beauty in the science and the beauty in the thing?<br /><br />Now, if only I can understand what Durant means by:” He was a Germanized Italian as Nietzsche was an Italianized German.” And why he assumes that this expression will carry meaning to his reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6568973311999677849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1251738948" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1251738948" />
    <a title="Corey Wozniak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17339120-corey-wozniak"><img alt="Corey Wozniak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360530485p2/17339120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1251738948?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corey Wozniak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corey Wozniak" href="/user/show/17339120-corey-wozniak">Corey Wozniak</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780671494155" href="/book/show/1770893.The_Story_Of_Philosophy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1251738948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7214150009627371545">The only fault of this book is the eye-straining font size; otherwise, this was a perfectly interesting and enlightening introduction to "the story of philosophy." Durrant is a terrific guide through the annals of philosophic history: he is witty, poetic, and erudite. Most important, he has a talent for simplicity without oversimplification. I appreciated that Durrant included long sections of the ver batim words of philosophers themselves. What I most liked is that he put flesh on the philosoph</span>
  <span id="freeText7214150009627371545" style="display:none">The only fault of this book is the eye-straining font size; otherwise, this was a perfectly interesting and enlightening introduction to "the story of philosophy." Durrant is a terrific guide through the annals of philosophic history: he is witty, poetic, and erudite. Most important, he has a talent for simplicity without oversimplification. I appreciated that Durrant included long sections of the ver batim words of philosophers themselves. What I most liked is that he put flesh on the philosophers. The biographic tidbits brought old Socrates and Voltaire to life. <br /><br />Perhaps the highest praise I can give this book is that it inspired me to seek out new books for further reading. Because of this book, I added books about/from Voltaire, Kant, Shopenhauer, Nietzsche, Bergson, James, and Dewey to my WANT TO READ list. <br /><br />All in all, I fell in love most with Voltaire, and especially look forward to reading his <i> Micromegas </i> (211-212) about alien visitors to earth, eternity, and perspective.<br /><br />A few quotes from my favorite philosophers:<br /><br /><b>VOLTAIRE: </b><br /><i> ON WAR </i><br />"Our God, who has bidden us love our enemies and suffer evil without complaint, assuredly has no mind that we should cross the sea to go and cut the throats of our brothers because murderers in red clothes and hats two feet high enlist citizens by making a nose with two sticks on an ass's skin" (207).<br /><i> ON LAUGHTER </i><br />"If Nature had not made us a little frivolous, we should be most wretched. It is because one can be frivolous that the majority do not hang themselves."<br />"Dulce est desipere in loco" [it is sweet to be foolish on occasion. Woe to philosophers who cannot laugh away their wrinkles. I look upon solemnity as a disease."<br /><br /><b>SHOPENHAUER: </b><br /><i> ON BOOKS </i><br />"Works [books] like this are as a mirror: if an ass looks in you cannot expect an angel to look out"; "when a head and a book come into collision, and one sounds hollow, is it always the book?"<br /><i> ON THE WORLD AS EVIL </i><br />"If we should bring clearly to a man's sight the terrible sufferings and misteries to which his life is constantly exposed, he would be seized with horror; and if we were to conduct the confirmed optimist through the hospitals, infirmaries, and surgical operating-rooms, through the prisons, torture-chamgers, and slave kennels, over battle-fields and places of execution... he too would understand at last the nature of this [evil world.] For whence did Dante take the materials of his hell but from our actual world? And yet he made a very proper hell out of it. But when, on the other hand, he came to describe heaven and its delights, he had an insurmountable difficulty before him, for our world affords no material at all for this."<br /><br /><b>BERGSON: </b><br /><i> Durrant, on Bergson's resistance to materialistic determinism </i><br />"Was determinism any more intelligible than free will? If the present moment contains no living and creative choice, and is totally and mechanically the product of the matter and motion of the moment before, then so was that moment the mechanical effect of the moment that preceded it, and that again of the one before...and so on, until we arrive at the primeval nebula as the toal cause of every later event, of every line of Shakespeare's plays, of every sufferent of his soul; so that the sombre rhetoric of Hamlet and Othello, of Macbeth and Lear, in every clause and every phrase, was written far off there in the distant skies and the distant aeons, by the structure and content of that legedary cloud. What a draft upon credulity! What an exercise of faith such a theory must demand on this unbelieving generation! What mystery or miracle, of Old Testament or New, could be half so incredible as this monstrous fatalistic myth, this nebular composing tragedies? There was matter enough for rebellion here; and if Bergson rose so rapidly to fame it was because he had the courage to doubt where all the doubters piously believed."<br /><i> ON MEMORY AND FREE WILL </i><br />""The primary function of memory is to evoke all those past perceptions which are analogous to the present perception, to recall to us what preceded and what followed them, and so to suggest to us that decision which is the most useful. But this is not all. But allowing us to grasp in a single intuition multiple moments of duration, it frees us from the movement of the flow of things, that is to say, from the rhythm of necessity. The more of these moments memory can contract into one, the firmer is the hold which it gives to us on matter; so that the memory of a living being appears indeed to measure, above all, its powers of action upon things."<br /><br /><b>James: </b><br /><i> ON THE REALITY OF THE UNSEEN WORLD </i><br />"I firmly disbelieve, myself, that our human experience is the highest form of experience extant in the universe. IO believe rather that we stand in much the same relation to the whole of the universe as our canine and feline pets do to the whole of human life. They inhabit our drawing rooms and libraries. They take part in scenes of whose significant they have no inkling. They are merely tangent to curves of history, the beginnings and ends and forms of which pass wholly beyond their ken. So we are tangent to the wider things of life."</span>

<div id="review_1717851106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1717851106" />
    <a title="Pavan Dharanipragada" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13424240-pavan-dharanipragada"><img alt="Pavan Dharanipragada" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377988340p2/13424240.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1717851106?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pavan Dharanipragada" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pavan Dharanipragada" href="/user/show/13424240-pavan-dharanipragada">Pavan Dharanipragada</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13424240-pavan-dharanipragada?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13424240-pavan-dharanipragada?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1717851106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8559501213987781037">Everyone is a philosopher in some capacity. We all have some belief as how the world works, how we should live and what we should live for. Some of us acquire it from parents or elders or other role-models or even movies. Some take it from religion. It's easy really, because it is passive mostly. But for others things start to lose sense. All the certainties they had in their youth start crumbling away. Or maybe they never were truly comfortable with the world they were living in. It is these pe</span>
  <span id="freeText8559501213987781037" style="display:none">Everyone is a philosopher in some capacity. We all have some belief as how the world works, how we should live and what we should live for. Some of us acquire it from parents or elders or other role-models or even movies. Some take it from religion. It's easy really, because it is passive mostly. But for others things start to lose sense. All the certainties they had in their youth start crumbling away. Or maybe they never were truly comfortable with the world they were living in. It is these personal crises that drive one to search for a new truth based on all the wonderful models of it made by her predecessors.<br /><br />So here we have comprehensive introductions to nine such models from history and their proponents, and shorter introductions to contemporary (in 1926) philosophers. We have the lives these masters led, their influences and their impact. We have everything important that they said and what they thought about everything important. You could see these were the minds that determined the course of western civilisation and represent a wide range of philosophies, including Eastern ones through a couple of proxies. <br /><br />Plato was the original master. He was extremely ambitious and he had the sort of command over everyone to realise his utopia. Every subsequent philosopher either reproduced his conclusions through their ideas or reacted strongly against his ideas. <br />Aristotle practically created logic. He drove Western philosophy for a long time; his theories were dogmatised by the Catholic Church and the aristocracy. <br />Francis Bacon fathered the scientific method and materialism and shaped England's destiny as the pioneer of industrial societies. <br />Spinoza was "the greatest philosopher of modern times". He sought to create a set of ethics not centered on God. "To be a philosopher, one must be a Spinozist first." <br />Voltaire taught us how to read history and why we should value liberty as our highest ideal; something we take for granted now. <br />Immanuel Kant was big on epistemology. He made us take several steps back in assuming what we know. He tried to make religion go back to its rightful space and not fight a battle with science it was certain to lose. <br />I loved Schopenhauer. He has just the kind of mix of depression and purpose in his writing that appeals to me. There's a lot of influence of Eastern, especially Buddhist, philosophy in his work. Maybe that's why it appealed to me so much. It's familiar. <br />Herbert Spencer was incredibly boring. His philosophy was based on the theory of evolution and it didn't do much for me. It felt like he lacked the conviction to take his premises to their logical conclusions which Nietzsche would do later. Apparently his celebrity dwindled soon after this book was written. <br />Friedrich Nietzsche's philosophy is deplorable but so very charismatic. It characterises all the horrors of late 19th and early 20th centuries. God is dead, he says. Of course, he didn't mean there was actually an entity alive called God who later died, but that we didn't need God for morality anymore and he sought to replace Him with his Übermensch. <br />Then there are Bergson, Croce and Bertrand Russell from Europe, and Santayana, William James and John Dewey from America. Important thinkers from 20th century. Dr. B R Ambedkar studied under John Dewey at Columbia University. <br /><br />Philosophy is ultimately generalisation. You pick the ones that work for you, make you happy and give a meaning to the chaos. Will Durant guides admirably in helping you understand the various philosophies you may take a shine to while gently nudging toward ones he values. Keep an open mind and jump into the shallows. Let the current that feels the strongest to you guide you to the depths.<br /><br />P. S. : This is my hundredth read book on Goodreads. Yay for an accomplishment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8559501213987781037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5MUVv6z1wzPzjA07LChWFpykaiXhtIOYtABW5T4bh80MGBAUYKvTLczW5dWV0xtthVpm82emr6qlfD0AJti8Fg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In 1958, a new translation by E. F. J. Payne appeared which decisively supplanted the older one. Payne\'s translation is superior because it corrects nearly 1,000 errors and omissions in the Haldane-Kemp translation, and it is based on the definitive 1937 German edition of Schopenhauer\'s work prepared by Dr. Arthur Hübscher. Followi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16254577153159594965\" style=\"display:none\">Published shortly after his death, the Ethics is undoubtedly Spinoza\'s greatest work - an elegant, fully cohesive cosmology derived from first principles, providing a coherent picture of reality, and a guide to the meaning of an ethical life. Following a logical step-by-step format, it defines in turn the nature of God, the mind, the emotions, human bondage to the emotions, and the power of understanding - moving from a consideration of the eternal, to speculate upon humanity\'s place in the natural order, the nature of freedom and the path to attainable happiness. A powerful work of elegant simplicity, the Ethics is a brilliantly insightful consideration of the possibility of redemption through intense thought and philosophical reflection. The Ethics is presented in the standard translation of the work by Edwin Curley. This edition also includes an introduction by Stuart Hampshire, outlining Spinoza\'s philosophy and placing it in context.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16254577153159594965\" href=\"#\" <script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_9570637'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9570637?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">قصة الفلسفة اليونانية<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2743905._?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">أحمد أمين<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.96 avg rating &mdash; 270 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1935\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer4447143039234054305\">قال الأستاذ أحمد أمين في مقدمة هذا الكتاب:\n\nلما عاودت القراءة في الفلسفة بدت مني رغبة في أن أكتب خلاصة ما أقرأ؛ فذلك أدعى إلى وضوح الفكرة في ذهني، وإلى أن يَنتفِع بما انتفعت به غيري. وكان من حسن حظي أن رأيتُ أخي وزميلي الأستاذ «زكي نجيب محمود» يرغب ر<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4447143039234054305\" style=\"display:none\">قال الأستاذ أحمد أمين في مقدمة هذا الكتاب:\n\nلما عاودت القراءة في الفلسفة بدت مني رغبة في أن أكتب خلاصة ما أقرأ؛ فذلك أدعى إلى وضوح الفكرة في ذهني، وإلى أن يَنتفِع بما انتفعت به غيري. وكان من حسن حظي أن رأيتُ أخي وزميلي الأستاذ «زكي نجيب محمود» يرغب رغبتي، ويتمنى أمنيتي، فتعاونَّا معًا على إخراج هذا الكتاب وتقديمه للقراء. وليس لنا فيه أفكار جديدة ولا آراء مبتكرة؛ فلسنا من علماء الفلسفة المتخصصين لها والمتبحرين فيها.\n\nوكل عملنا أننا قرأنا الموضوع في كتب متعددة وأخذنا خلاصتها، وصغناه صياغة أقرب إلى ذهن القارئ العربي، وتخيرنا ما هو أنسب له وأقرب إلى ذوقه. فليس عملنا تأليفًا بالمعنى الدقيق للتأليف، ولا ترجمة بالمعنى الدقيق للترجمة؛ ولذلك اخترنا للدلالة على ما عملناه كلمة تصنيف، فلعلها أدل على القصد، وأصدق في الوصف.\n\nراعينا فيه قدر الإمكان وضوح الفكرة، وبساطة التعبير، حتى أسميناه قصة، كما راعينا الاقتصار على أهم المسائل وأصولها دون التوسع في فروعها وجزئياتها.\nوهذا الكلام حسن الدلالة على هذا الكتاب النافع الجيد، ونرى أن حقيقة الكتاب أكبر مما قضى به «تواضع» الأستاذين في المقدمة؛ فهو أصح الخلاصات عن الفلسفة اليونانية في اللغة العربية، وهو واضح العبارة، حسن الترتيب والانتقاء لما هو أولى بالتدوين في الخلاصات الوجيزة، وهو وافي المراجع، كثير الأصول، بل ربما كانت مراجعه وأصوله أكثر مما هو لازم لكتاب في حجمه، وسيحبب الفلسفة إلى قرائه، ويرغبهم في استقصاء المطولات ومتابعة التفكير في هذه المسائل، ويؤدي واجبًا لا بد من أدائه في نهضة الثقافة الحديثة بالبلاد العربية.\n\nولنا ملاحظة خاصة بعد هذه الملاحظة العامة نبديها على النمط الذي قرر به الأستاذان ابتداء الفلسفة في بلاد اليونان، فقد قالا في الصفحة السادسة عشرة:\n\nنعم كان في مصر طائفة كبيرة من العقائد تدور حول النفس وما يطرأ على الحياة بعد الموت، ولكن لم يَثبُتْ إنْ كان لديها من العلوم الإيجابية النظرية شيء كثير. ولو عرف المصريون كثيرًا من علوم الرياضة لما رأينا في كتب فيثاغورس محاولات أولية للهندسة، مع العلم بأن عهده في التاريخ جاء بعد اتصال اليونان بالمصريين اتصالًا وثيقًا، واستمدادهم من المصريين بعض معارفهم وحضارتهم، فليست القواعد العملية التي استعملها المصريون في أغراضهم كقياس الأرض وبناء الأهرام هي العلم الذي قصده كورنيكس، وجاليليو، وكبلر، ونيوتن.\n\nلم تستمد الفلسفة اليونانية أصولها من تلك الأمم القديمة، ولكن خلقها اليونان خلقًا وأنشئوها إنشاءً، فهي وليدتهم وربيبتهم، وليس في ذلك ريب ولا شك. ويستطيع الباحث أن يرجع بالفلسفة خطوة بعد خطوة حتى يصل إلى مهدها في بلاد اليونان دون أن يشعر خلال البحث بحلقة مفقودة أو غامضة.\n\nونحن إذا ذكرنا بلاد اليونان في هذا المقام لا نقصر هذا الاسم على هذه البلاد التي تسمى به اليوم فحسب، إنما نضيف إليها المستعمرات اليونانية — وهي في الواقع مهد الفلسفة — فقد بسط اليونان نفوذهم ونشروا سلطانهم في آسيا الصغرى، وجزيرة صقلية، وجنوبي إيطاليا، وجزء من شمالي أفريقيا. في تلك المستعمرات ولدت الفلسفة وشبَّت قبل أن تنتقل إلى أرض اليونان نفسها.\nفهذا التقرير على صحته في الجملة لا يورَد في كتاب فلسفي على هذا النمط، ولو كان مؤلفه من غير المصريين أو الشرقيين على أوسع تعميم؛ لأن إيراد حكم بغير تعليل يناقض الفلسفة التي نعرفها لنعرف أسباب الأشياء، ونمنع بها إلقاء الأحكام بلا قياس.\n\nفليست النحيزة المصرية وحدها هي التي تقضي على مؤرخ الفلسفة أن يعلل انفراد اليونان بالتفكير الفلسفي في العصور القديمة، وإنما الفلسفة نفسها هي التي توجب هذا التعليل وتمنع إرساله على هذا النحو المعطل للتفكير.\n\nوعندنا — كما أسلفنا في بعض كتاباتنا — أن اليونان لم ينفردوا بالفلسفة قديمًا لأن الفكر الإنساني فيهم «نوع مستقل» يخالف نوع الفكر الإنساني في جملة مزاياه، ولكنهم انفردوا بها لأنهم أمة ضعيفة لم تنشأ فيها دولة قوية مستقرة، وهم مع ذلك مقيمون في العمار غير مرتحلين كما يرتحل أبناء القبائل البدويون.\n\nفالمصريون نشأت لهم دولة قوية، فنشأت مع الدولة كهانة قوية، ومن دأب الكهانات القوية أن تسيطر على العقائد والمباحث في أصول الأشياء؛ لأن هذه المباحث تمس — أول ما تمس — جوهر الدين والاعتقاد بالخلق والخالق والفرائض والعبادات؛ فيستولي الكُهَّان على مذاهب التفكير ويصبغونها — كما هو شأنهم — بصبغة المراسم الموروثة والشعائر المقدسة، ويخرجون بها من عالم الفلسفة الطلقة إلى عالم الحدود والحُرُمات الممنوعة.\n\nأما اليونان فلم تكن لهم دولة مستقرة، ولم ينشأ عندهم ما يمنع انطلاق الأفكار في وجهتها الفطرية التي فطر عليها جميع الناس، ولو نشأت عندهم الدولة، ونشأت مع الدولة الكهانة؛ لما خاض فلاسفتهم وحكماؤهم فيما خاضوا فيه، واستأثر بأمره جماعة الكهان والسدنة في الشعوب الأخرى.\n\nوآية ذلك أن اليونان — والغربيين عامة — أحجموا عن خوض المباحث الفلسفية حين قامت بينهم الكهانة القوية في بيزنطة وروما، وسيطرت على مذاهب الفكر، وأدخلت كل رأي وكل علم وكل فن في حظيرة الدين.\n\nفحالة الغربيين في ظل الكهانة الغالبة لم تكن تختلف عن حالة المصريين الأقدمين أو الفرس أو الهنود أو الكلدان؛ حيث تجري الأنهار، ويستقر العمران، ويتوطد الملك ومعه سلطان الكهانات.\n\nوكذلك شاع التفكير لحب التفكير، والبحث في العلوم لحب العلوم حين قامت بين الشرقيين دولة كالدولة العباسية لا تقترن بها كهانة كالتي كانت تقترن بالأديان الأخرى؛ فاخترع العرب والشرقيون ما اخترعوه، وكشفوا ما كشفوه في علوم الطبيعة والفلك والرياضة، كما كان اليونان الأقدمون يخترعون ويكشفون.\n\nتلك هي العلة ولا علة غيرها. قد يروق المؤرخين للفلسفة بين الغربيين أن يسكتوا عنها ولا يحرصوا على نشدانها؛ لأنهم يبنون على انفراد اليونان بالفلسفة دعوى تشرف عنصرهم، وتعظم عقولهم وطبائعهم، ويتخذون منها برهانًا على رجحان يزعمونه في معدن العقول الغربية على معدن العقول الشرقية من قديمة وحديثة، وذريعة إلى طلب السيادة وتقرير الفوارق الجنسية، ولكن المؤرخ الشرقي لا يُرضي الفلسفة والتفكير، ولا يُرضي نفسه ولا قومه بالسكوت عن هذه العلة، وهي أقرب إلى مَن يطلبها من أن تُنسى هذا       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/16184.Will_Durant">About Will Durant</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12440269486878012965">William James Durant was a prolific American writer, historian, and philosopher. He is best known for the 11-volume The Story of Civilization, written in collaboration with his wife Ariel and published between 1935 and 1975. He was earlier noted for his book, The Story of Philosophy, written in 1926, which was considered "a groundbreaking work that helped to popularize philosophy."<br/><br/>They were awarde</span>
  <span id="freeText12440269486878012965" style="display:none">William James Durant was a prolific American writer, historian, and philosopher. He is best known for the 11-volume The Story of Civilization, written in collaboration with his wife Ariel and published between 1935 and 1975. He was earlier noted for his book, The Story of Philosophy, written in 1926, which was considered "a groundbreaking work that helped to popularize philosophy."<br /><br />They were awarded the Pulitzer Prize for literature in 1967 and the Presidential Medal of Freedom in 1977.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12440269486878012965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
